<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Southern Ocean</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Location_Southern_Ocean.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="541" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Location_Southern_Ocean.svg/300px-Location_Southern_Ocean.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Location_Southern_Ocean.svg/450px-Location_Southern_Ocean.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Location_Southern_Ocean.svg/600px-Location_Southern_Ocean.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Location_Southern_Ocean.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Southern Ocean, as delineated by the draft 4th edition of the <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a>'s <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i> (2002).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica-Region.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="874" data-file-width="874" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Antarctica-Region.png/300px-Antarctica-Region.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Antarctica-Region.png/450px-Antarctica-Region.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Antarctica-Region.png/600px-Antarctica-Region.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica-Region.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A general delineation of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a>, sometimes used by scientists as the demarcation of the Southern Ocean.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Southern Ocean</b>, also known as the <b>Antarctic Ocean</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27EB.27.271878_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27EB.27.271878-1">[1]</a></sup> or the <b>Austral Ocean</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENGS2014_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENGS2014-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[note 4]</a></sup> comprises the southernmost waters of the <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>, generally taken to be south of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞¬†S latitude</a> and encircling <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAgeo_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAgeo-10">[6]</a></sup> As such, it is regarded as the fourth-largest of the five principal <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> divisions: smaller than the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Oceans</a> but larger than the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAintro_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAintro-11">[7]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_zone" title="Oceanic zone">ocean zone</a> is where cold, northward flowing waters from the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> mix with warmer <a href="/wiki/Subantarctic" title="Subantarctic">subantarctic</a> waters.</p>
<p>By way of his voyages in the 1770s, <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">Captain James Cook</a> proved that waters encompassed the southern latitudes of the globe. Since then, geographers have disagreed on the Southern Ocean's northern boundary or even existence, considering the waters part of the Pacific, Atlantic, and Indian Oceans, instead. This remains the current official policy of the <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> (IHO), since a 2000 revision of its definitions including the Southern Ocean as the waters south of the 60th parallel has not yet been adopted. Others regard the seasonally-fluctuating <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a> as the natural boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_use">Definitions and use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions and use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:514px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iceedgekils.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="512" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Iceedgekils.gif" width="512"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iceedgekils.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pulsating pack-ice around Antarctica reflects the various definitions of the Southern Ocean</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Borders and names for oceans and seas were internationally agreed when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Bureau" title="International Hydrographic Bureau">International Hydrographic Bureau</a> (IHB), the precusor to the IHO, convened the First International Conference on 24 July 1919. The IHO then published these in its <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>, the first edition being 1928. Since the first edition, the limits of the Southern Ocean have moved progressively southwards; since 1953, it has been omitted from the official publication and left to local hydrographic offices to determine their own limits. The IHO included the ocean and its definition as the waters south of 60¬∞S in its year 2000 revisions, but this has not been formally adopted, due to continuing impasses over other areas of the text, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan_naming_dispute" title="Sea of Japan naming dispute">naming dispute</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a>. The 2000 IHO definition, however, was circulated in a draft edition in 2002 and is used by some within the IHO and by some other organizations such as the US <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAintro_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAintro-11">[7]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster_Dictionary" title="Merriam-Webster Dictionary">Merriam-Webster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[note 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merriamwebster_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-merriamwebster-14">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> authorities regard the Southern Ocean as lying immediately south of Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-Indian_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Indian-16">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a> does not recognize the ocean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENGS2014_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENGS2014-2">[2]</a></sup> depicting it (if at all) in a typeface different from the other world oceans; instead, it shows the Pacific, Atlantic, and Indian Oceans extending to Antarctica on both its print and on line maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-17">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[note 6]</a></sup> Map publishers using the term Southern Ocean on their maps include Hema Maps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HemaMaps_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-HemaMaps-20">[14]</a></sup> and GeoNova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeoNova_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeoNova-21">[15]</a></sup></p>
<table>
<tr valign="top">
<td>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7500" data-file-width="15000" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png/800px-The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png/1200px-The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png/1600px-The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png 2x" width="800"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_shrinking_Southern_Ocean.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a>'s delineation of the "Southern Ocean" has moved steadily southwards since the original 1928 edition of its <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>. <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> continues to view the ocean as beginning at its southern coast. The 1953 limits shown are those of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a>, as identified in third edition. Others continue to view the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a> as the natural boundary of the Southern Ocean, regardless of political agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAgeo_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAgeo-10">[6]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-20th-century_definitions">Pre-20th-century definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-20th-century definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_new_map_of_Africk,_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns,_rivers,_mountain_%26c._(8250932292).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1405" data-file-width="2000" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg/220px-A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg/330px-A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg/440px-A_new_map_of_Africk%2C_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns%2C_rivers%2C_mountain_%26c._%288250932292%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_new_map_of_Africk,_shewing_its_present_general_divisions_cheif_cities_or_towns,_rivers,_mountain_%26c._(8250932292).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Southern Ocean" as an alternative name for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aethiopian_Ocean" title="Aethiopian Ocean">Aethiopian Ocean</a> in a 1700 map of Africa</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"Southern Ocean" is an obsolete name for the Pacific Ocean or South Pacific, coined by <a href="/wiki/Vasco_N%C3%BA%C3%B1ez_de_Balboa" title="Vasco N√∫√±ez de Balboa">Vasco N√∫√±ez de Balboa</a>, the first European to discover it, who approached it from the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[16]</a></sup> The "South Seas" is a less archaic synonym. A 1745 British <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> established a prize for discovering a <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a> to "the Western and Southern Ocean of <i>America</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritainTomlins1811_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritainTomlins1811-23">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Authors using "Southern Ocean" to name the waters encircling the unknown southern polar regions used varying limits. <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>'s account of <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="Second voyage of James Cook">his second voyage</a> implies <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a> borders it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook1821_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook1821-24">[18]</a></sup> Peacock's 1795 <i>Geographical Dictionary</i> said it lay "to the southward of America and Africa";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[19]</a></sup> John Payne in 1796 used 40 degrees as the northern limit;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Payne1796_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Payne1796-26">[20]</a></sup> the 1827 <i>Edinburgh Gazetteer</i> used 50 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[21]</a></sup> The <i>Family Magazine</i> in 1835 divided the "Great Southern Ocean" into the "Southern Ocean" and the "Antarctick [<i>sic</i>] Ocean" along the Antarctic Circle, with the northern limit of the Southern Ocean being lines joining Cape Horn, the Cape of Good Hope, Van Diemen's Land and the south of New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/South_Australia_Act_1834" title="South Australia Act 1834">South Australia Act 1834</a> described the waters forming the southern limit of the new colony of <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> as "the Southern Ocean". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colony_of_Victoria" title="Colony of Victoria">Colony of Victoria</a>'s Legislative Council Act of 1881 delimited part of the division of <a href="/wiki/Bairnsdale" title="Bairnsdale">Bairnsdale</a> as "along the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> boundary to the Southern ocean".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1928_delineation">1928 delineation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1928 delineation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6380" data-file-width="10065" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg/220px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg/330px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg/440px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_1st_Edition_-_1928.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>1928 First Edition</b> of <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i> with original IHO delineation of Southern Ocean abutting land-masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOO1928_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAOO1928-30">[24]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1928 first edition of <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>, the Southern Ocean was delineated by land-based limits: Antarctica to the south, and South America, Africa, Australia, and <a href="/wiki/Broughton_Island,_New_Zealand" title="Broughton Island, New Zealand">Broughton Island, New Zealand</a> to the north.</p>
<p>The detailed land-limits used were from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> in South America eastwards to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a> in Africa, then further eastwards to the southern coast of mainland Australia to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Leeuwin" title="Cape Leeuwin">Cape Leeuwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>. From Cape Leeuwin, the limit then followed eastwards along the coast of mainland Australia to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Otway" title="Cape Otway">Cape Otway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>, then southwards across <a href="/wiki/Bass_Strait" title="Bass Strait">Bass Strait</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Wickham" title="Cape Wickham">Cape Wickham</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Island_(Tasmania)" title="King Island (Tasmania)">King Island</a>, along the west coast of King Island, then the remainder of the way south across Bass Strait to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Grim" title="Cape Grim">Cape Grim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>. The limit then followed the west coast of Tasmania southwards to the <a href="/wiki/South_East_Cape" title="South East Cape">South East Cape</a> and then went eastwards to Broughton Island, New Zealand, before returning to Cape Horn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOO1928_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAOO1928-30">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7888" data-file-width="9968" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg/220px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg/330px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg/440px-Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Limit_of_Oceans_and_Seas_-_2nd_Edition_-_1st_July_1937.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>1937 Second Edition</b> of <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i> showing IHO's pre-1953 delineation of Southern Ocean moved southwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOO1937_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAOO1937-31">[25]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1937_delineation">1937 delineation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1937 delineation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The northern limits of the Southern Ocean were moved southwards in the IHO's 1937 second edition of the <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>. From this edition, much of the ocean's northern limit ceased to abut land masses.</p>
<p>In the second edition, the Southern Ocean then extended from Antarctica northwards to latitude 40¬∞S between <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a> in Africa (long. 20¬∞E) and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Leeuwin" title="Cape Leeuwin">Cape Leeuwin</a> in Western Australia (long. 115¬∞E), and extended to latitude 55¬∞S between <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Island" title="Auckland Island">Auckland Island</a> of New Zealand (165 or 166¬∞E east) and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> in South America (67¬∞W).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOO1937_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAOO1937-31">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>As is discussed in more detail below (see section on '2002 delineation'), prior to the 2002 (draft) edition the limits of oceans explicitly excluded the seas lying within each of them. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a> was unnamed in the 1928 edition, and delineated as shown in the figure above in the 1937 edition. It therefore encompassed former Southern Ocean waters (as designated in 1928) but was technically not inside any of the three adjacent oceans by 1937. In the 2002 draft edition, the IHO have designated 'seas' as being subdivisions within 'oceans', so the Bight would have still been within the Southern Ocean in 1937 if the 2002 convention were in place then. To perform direct comparisons of current and former limits of oceans (for example to compare surface areas) it is necessary to consider, or at least be aware of, how the 2002 change in IHO terminology for 'seas' can affect the comparison.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1953_delineation">1953 delineation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1953 delineation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Southern Ocean did not appear in the 1953 third edition and a note in the publication read:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Antarctic or Southern Ocean has been omitted from this publication as the majority of opinions received since the issue of the 2nd Edition in 1937 are to the effect that there exists no real justification for applying the term Ocean to this body of water, the northern limits of which are difficult to lay down owing to their seasonal change. The limits of the Atlantic, Pacific and Indian Oceans have therefore been extended South to the Antarctic Continent.</p>
<p>Hydrographic Offices who issue separate publications dealing with this area are therefore left to decide their own northern limits (Great Britain uses Latitude of 55 South.)</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Instead, in the IHO 1953 publication, the Atlantic, Indian and Pacific Oceans were extended southward, the Indian and Pacific Oceans (which had not previously touched pre 1953, as per the first and second editions) now abutted at the meridian of <a href="/wiki/South_East_Cape" title="South East Cape">South East Cape</a>, and the southern limits of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tasman_Sea" title="Tasman Sea">Tasman Sea</a> were moved northwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-32">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="330" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Pacific_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png/250px-Pacific_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/Pacific_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png 1.5x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <b>Pacific Ocean</b> as example of terminology concerning seas: the area inside the black line includes the seas included in the Pacific Ocean prior to 2002 and the darker blue areas are its informal current borders, following the recreation of the Southern Ocean and the reinclusion of marginal seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2002_.28draft.29_delineation">2002 (draft) delineation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2002 (draft) delineation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The IHO readdressed the question of the Southern Ocean in a survey in 2000. Of its 68 member nations, 28 responded, and all responding members except <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> agreed to redefine the ocean, reflecting the importance placed by oceanographers on ocean currents. The proposal for the name <i>Southern Ocean</i> won 18 votes, beating the alternative <i>Antarctic Ocean</i>. Half of the votes supported a definition of the ocean's northern limit at <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞S</a> (with no land interruptions at this latitude), with the other 14 votes cast for other definitions, mostly <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50¬∞S</a>, but a few for as far north as <a href="/wiki/35th_parallel_south" title="35th parallel south">35¬∞S</a>.</p>
<p>A draft fourth edition of <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i> was circulated to IHO member states in August 2002 (sometimes referred to as the "2000 edition" as it summarized the progress to 2000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO2002_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO2002-34">[28]</a></sup> It has yet to be published due to 'areas of concern' by several countries relating to various naming issues around the world ‚Äì primarily the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan_naming_dispute" title="Sea of Japan naming dispute">Sea of Japan naming dispute</a> ‚Äì and there have been various changes, 60 seas were given new names, and even the name of the publication was changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[29]</a></sup> A reservation had also been lodged by Australia regarding the Southern Ocean limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[30]</a></sup> Effectively, the 3rd edition (which did not delineate the Southern Ocean leaving delineation to local hydrographic offices) has yet to be superseded.</p>
<p>Despite this, the 4th edition definition has partial <i>de facto</i> usage by many nations, scientists and organisations such as the U.S. (the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Factbook" title="World Factbook">World Factbook</a> uses "Southern Ocean" but none of the other new sea names within the "Southern Ocean" such as "<a href="/wiki/Cosmonauts_Sea" title="Cosmonauts Sea">Cosmonauts Sea</a>") and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster_Dictionary" title="Merriam-Webster Dictionary">Merriam-Webster</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAintro_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAintro-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merriamwebster_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-merriamwebster-14">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic-18">[13]</a></sup> scientists and nations ‚Äì and even by some within the IHO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[31]</a></sup> Some nations' hydrographic offices have defined their own boundaries; the United Kingdom used the <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55¬∞S parallel</a> for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-32">[26]</a></sup> Other organisations favour more northerly limits for the Southern Ocean. For example, the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica,_Inc." title="Encyclop√¶dia Britannica, Inc.">Encyclop√¶dia Britannica</a></i> describes the Southern Ocean as extending as far north as South America, and confers great significance on the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a>, yet its description of the Indian Ocean contradicts this, describing the Indian Ocean as extending south to Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-Indian_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Indian-16">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-Southern_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Southern-38">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other sources, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, show the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a> Oceans as extending to Antarctica on its maps, although articles on the National Geographic web site have begun to reference the Southern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic-18">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographical</a> authorities define the Southern Ocean as including the entire body of water between Antarctica and the south coasts of Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and up to 60¬∞s elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahs_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahs-39">[33]</a></sup> Coastal maps of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> label the sea areas as <i>Southern Ocean</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[34]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Cape_Leeuwin" title="Cape Leeuwin">Cape Leeuwin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> is described as the point where the Indian and Southern Oceans meet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>A radical shift from past IHO practices (1928‚Äì1953) was also seen in the 2002 draft edition when the IHO delineated 'seas' as being subdivisions that lay within the boundaries of 'oceans'. While the IHO are often considered the authority for such conventions, the shift brought them into line with the practices of other publications (e.g. the CIA <i>World Fact Book</i>) which already adopted the principle that seas are contained within oceans. This difference in practice is markedly seen for the <b>Pacific Ocean</b> in the adjacent figure. Thus, for example, previously the <a href="/wiki/Tasman_Sea" title="Tasman Sea">Tasman Sea</a> between Australia and New Zealand was not regarded by the IHO as being part of the Pacific, but as of the 2002 draft edition it is.</p>
<p>The new delineation of seas being subdivisions of oceans has avoided the need to interrupt the northern boundary of the Southern Ocean where intersected by <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> which includes all of the waters from South America to the Antarctic coast, nor interrupt it for the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Sea" title="Scotia Sea">Scotia Sea</a>, which also extends below the 60th parallel south. The new delineation of seas has also meant that the long-time named seas around Antarctica, excluded from the 1953 edition (the 1953 map did not even extend that far south), are 'automatically' part of the Southern Ocean.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_exploration">History of exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History of exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Antarctica" title="History of Antarctica">History of Antarctica</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_unknown_southern_land">The unknown southern land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The unknown southern land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3961" data-file-width="5816" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg/220px-OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg/330px-OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg/440px-OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OrteliusWorldMap1570.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1564 <i>Typus Orbis Terrarum</i>, a map by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> showed the imagined link between the proposed continent of Antarctica and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Exploration of the Southern Ocean was inspired by a belief in the existence of a <i>Terra Australis</i>‚Äîa vast continent in the far south of the globe to "balance" the northern lands of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>‚Äîwhich had existed since the times of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>. The doubling of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> in 1487 by <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> first brought explorers within touch of the Antarctic cold, and proved that there was an ocean separating <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> from any Antarctic land that might exist. <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Ferdinand Magellan</a>, who passed through the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Magellan" title="Strait of Magellan">Strait of Magellan</a> in 1520, assumed that the islands of <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> to the south were an extension of this unknown southern land. In 1564, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> published his first map, <i>Typus Orbis Terrarum</i>, an eight-leaved wall map of the world, on which he identified the <i><a href="/wiki/Regio_Patalis" title="Regio Patalis">Regio Patalis</a></i> with <i><a href="/wiki/Locach" title="Locach">Locach</a></i> as a northward extension of the <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a></i>, reaching as far as <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-42">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>European geographers continued to connect the coast of Tierra del Fuego with the coast of New Guinea on their globes, and allowing their imaginations to run riot in the vast unknown spaces of the south Atlantic, south <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> oceans they sketched the outlines of the <i>Terra Australis Incognita</i> ("Unknown Southern Land"), a vast continent stretching in parts into the tropics. The search for this great south land was a leading motive of explorers in the 16th and the early part of the 17th centuries.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spaniard</a> <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_de_Castilla" title="Gabriel de Castilla">Gabriel de Castilla</a>, who claimed having sighted "snow-covered mountains" beyond the <a href="/wiki/64th_parallel_south" title="64th parallel south">64¬∞ S</a> in 1603, is recognized as the first explorer that discovered the continent of Antarctica, although he was ignored in his time.</p>
<p>In 1606, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedro_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Quir%C3%B3s" title="Pedro Fern√°ndez de Quir√≥s">Pedro Fern√°ndez de Quir√≥s</a> took possession for the king of Spain all of the lands he had discovered in Australia del Espiritu Santo (the <a href="/wiki/New_Hebrides" title="New Hebrides">New Hebrides</a>) and those he would discover "even to the Pole".</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a>, like Spanish explorers before him, had speculated that there might be an open channel south of Tierra del Fuego. When <a href="/wiki/Willem_Schouten" title="Willem Schouten">Willem Schouten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Le_Maire" title="Jacob Le Maire">Jacob Le Maire</a> discovered the southern extremity of Tierra del Fuego and named it <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> in 1615, they proved that the Tierra del Fuego archipelago was of small extent and not connected to the southern land, as previously thought. Subsequently, in 1642, <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> showed that even <a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland (Australia)</a> was separated by sea from any continuous southern continent.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edmund_Halley.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="1000" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Edmund_Halley.gif/220px-Edmund_Halley.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Edmund_Halley.gif/330px-Edmund_Halley.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Edmund_Halley.gif/440px-Edmund_Halley.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edmund_Halley.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_Halley" title="Edmund Halley">Edmund Halley</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Murray_(artist)" title="Thomas Murray (artist)">Thomas Murray</a>, c. 1687</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_of_the_Antarctic_Convergence">South of the Antarctic Convergence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: South of the Antarctic Convergence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anthony_de_la_Roch%C3%A9" title="Anthony de la Roch√©">Anthony de la Roch√©</a></div>
<p>The visit to <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_de_la_Roch%C3%A9" title="Anthony de la Roch√©">Anthony de la Roch√©</a> in 1675 was the first ever discovery of land south of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a> i.e. in the Southern Ocean / <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[39]</a></sup> Soon after the voyage cartographers started to depict ‚Äò<a href="/wiki/Anthony_de_la_Roch%C3%A9#Maps_showing_la_Roch.C3.A9.27s_discovery" title="Anthony de la Roch√©">Roch√© Island</a>‚Äô, honouring the discoverer. <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> was aware of la Roch√©'s discovery when surveying and mapping the island in 1775.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a>'s voyage in <a href="/wiki/HMS_Paramour_(1694)" title="HMS Paramour (1694)">HMS <i>Paramour</i></a> for magnetic investigations in the South Atlantic met the pack ice in <a href="/wiki/52nd_parallel_south" title="52nd parallel south">52¬∞¬†S</a> in January 1700, but that latitude (he reached 140¬†mi off the north coast of <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a>) was his farthest south. A determined effort on the part of the French naval officer <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Charles_Bouvet_de_Lozier" title="Jean-Baptiste Charles Bouvet de Lozier">Jean-Baptiste Charles Bouvet de Lozier</a> to discover the "South Land" ‚Äì described by a half legendary "sieur de Gonneyville" ‚Äì resulted in the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a> in 54¬∞10‚Ä≤¬†S, and in the navigation of <a href="/wiki/48th_meridian_west" title="48th meridian west">48¬∞ of longitude</a> of ice-cumbered sea nearly in <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55¬∞¬†S</a> in 1730 .</p>
<p>In 1771, <a href="/wiki/Yves-Joseph_de_Kerguelen-Tr%C3%A9marec" title="Yves-Joseph de Kerguelen-Tr√©marec">Yves Joseph Kerguelen</a> sailed from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> with instructions to proceed south from <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> in search of "a very large continent." He lighted upon a land in <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50¬∞¬†S</a> which he called South France, and believed to be the central mass of the southern continent. He was sent out again to complete the exploration of the new land, and found it to be only an inhospitable island which he renamed the Isle of Desolation, but which <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Islands" title="Kerguelen Islands">was ultimately named after him</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-47">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_of_the_Antarctic_Circle">South of the Antarctic Circle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: South of the Antarctic Circle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captainjamescookportrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1020" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/220px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/330px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/440px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captainjamescookportrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Famous official portrait of Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> who proved that waters encompassed the southern latitudes of the globe. <i>"He holds his own chart of the Southern Ocean on the table and his right hand points to the east coast of Australia on it."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geography_world_map.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="4420" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Geography_world_map.jpeg/250px-Geography_world_map.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Geography_world_map.jpeg/375px-Geography_world_map.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Geography_world_map.jpeg/500px-Geography_world_map.jpeg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geography_world_map.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map from 1771, showing "Terres Australes" (sic) label without any charted landmass.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="158" data-file-width="300" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg/250px-James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of <a href="/wiki/James_Weddell" title="James Weddell">James Weddell</a>'s second expedition in 1823, depicting the brig <i>Jane</i> and the cutter <i>Beaufroy</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The obsession of the undiscovered continent culminated in the brain of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Dalrymple" title="Alexander Dalrymple">Alexander Dalrymple</a>, the brilliant and erratic <a href="/wiki/Hydrography" title="Hydrography">hydrographer</a> who was nominated by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> to command the <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Transit of Venus</a> expedition to <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> in 1769. The command of the expedition was given by the admiralty to Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>. Sailing in 1772 with the <i>Resolution</i>, a vessel of 462 tons under his own command and the <i>Adventure</i> of 336 tons under Captain <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Furneaux" title="Tobias Furneaux">Tobias Furneaux</a>, Cook first searched in vain for <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a>, then sailed for 20 degrees of longitude to the westward in <a href="/wiki/58th_parallel_south" title="58th parallel south">latitude 58¬∞¬†S</a>, and then 30¬∞ eastward for the most part south of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞¬†S</a>, a lower southern latitude than had ever been voluntarily entered before by any vessel. On 17 January 1773 the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a> was crossed for the first time in history and the two ships reached <span class="nowrap">67¬∞ 15' S</span> by <span class="nowrap">39¬∞ 35' E</span>, where their course was stopped by ice.</p>
<p>Cook then turned northward to look for <a href="/wiki/French_Southern_and_Antarctic_Lands" title="French Southern and Antarctic Lands">French Southern and Antarctic Lands</a>, of the discovery of which he had received news at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, but from the rough determination of his longitude by Kerguelen, Cook reached the assigned latitude 10¬∞ too far east and did not see it. He turned south again and was stopped by ice in <span class="nowrap">61¬∞ 52‚Ä≤ S</span> by 95¬∞¬†E and continued eastward nearly on the parallel of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞¬†S</a> to <a href="/wiki/147th_meridian_east" title="147th meridian east">147¬∞¬†E</a>. On 16 March, the approaching winter drove him northward for rest to <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and the tropical islands of the Pacific. In November 1773, Cook left New Zealand, having parted company with the <i>Adventure</i>, and reached <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞¬†S</a> by <a href="/wiki/177th_meridian_west" title="177th meridian west">177¬∞¬†W</a>, whence he sailed eastward keeping as far south as the floating ice allowed. The Antarctic Circle was crossed on 20 December and Cook remained south of it for three days, being compelled after reaching <span class="nowrap">67¬∞ 31‚Ä≤ S</span> to stand north again in <a href="/wiki/135th_meridian_west" title="135th meridian west">135¬∞¬†W</a>.</p>
<p>A long detour to <span class="nowrap">47¬∞ 50‚Ä≤ S</span> served to show that there was no land connection between New Zealand and <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a>. Turning south again, Cook crossed the Antarctic Circle for the third time at <span class="nowrap">109¬∞ 30‚Ä≤ W</span> before his progress was once again blocked by ice four days later at <span class="nowrap">71¬∞ 10‚Ä≤ S</span> by <span class="nowrap">106¬∞ 54‚Ä≤ W</span>. This point, reached on 30 January 1774, was the farthest south attained in the 18th century. With a great detour to the east, almost to the coast of South America, the expedition regained Tahiti for refreshment. In November 1774, Cook started from New Zealand and crossed the South Pacific without sighting land between <a href="/wiki/53rd_parallel_south" title="53rd parallel south">53¬∞</a> and <a href="/wiki/57th_parallel_south" title="57th parallel south">57¬∞¬†S</a> to Tierra del Fuego; then, passing Cape Horn on 29 December, he rediscovered <a href="/wiki/Anthony_de_la_Roch%C3%A9#Maps_showing_la_Roch.C3.A9.27s_discovery" title="Anthony de la Roch√©">Roch√© Island</a> renaming it <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">Isle of Georgia</a>, and discovered the <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Sandwich Islands</a> (named <i>Sandwich Land</i> by him), the only ice-clad land he had seen, before crossing the South Atlantic to the Cape of Good Hope between <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55¬∞</a> and <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞</a>. He thereby laid open the way for future Antarctic exploration by exploding the myth of a habitable southern continent. Cook's most southerly discovery of land lay on the temperate side of the <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60th parallel</a>, and he convinced himself that if land lay farther south it was practically inaccessible and without economic value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-47">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Voyagers rounding the Horn frequently met with contrary winds and were driven southward into snowy skies and ice-encumbered seas; but so far as can be ascertained none of them before 1770 reached the Antarctic Circle, or knew it, if they did.</p>
<p>In a voyage from 1822 to 1824, <a href="/wiki/James_Weddell" title="James Weddell">James Weddell</a> commanded the 160-ton <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> <i>Jane</i>, accompanied by his second ship <i>Beaufoy</i> captained by Matthew Brisbane. Together they sailed to the South Orkneys where <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">sealing</a> proved disappointing. They turned south in the hope of finding a better sealing ground. The season was unusually mild and tranquil, and on 20 February 1823 the two ships reached <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> 74¬∞15'¬†S and <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> 34¬∞16'45‚Ä≥¬†W the southernmost position any ship had ever reached up to that time. A few icebergs were sighted but there was still no sight of land, leading Weddell to theorize that the sea continued as far as the South Pole. Another two days' sailing would have brought him to <a href="/wiki/Coats_Land" title="Coats Land">Coat's Land</a> (to the east of the <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">Weddell Sea</a>) but Weddell decided to turn back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_sighting_of_land">First sighting of land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: First sighting of land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="449" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg/220px-Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg/330px-Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg/440px-Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Faddey_Faddeyevich_Bellingshausen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Admiral von Bellingshausen</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(mariner)" title="William Smith (mariner)">William Smith (mariner)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabian_von_Bellingshausen" title="Fabian von Bellingshausen">Fabian von Bellingshausen</a></div>
<p>The first land south of the <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">parallel 60¬∞ south latitude</a> was discovered by the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">Englishman</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(mariner)" title="William Smith (mariner)">William Smith</a>, who sighted <a href="/wiki/Livingston_Island" title="Livingston Island">Livingston Island</a> on 19 February 1819. A few months later Smith returned to explore the other islands of the <a href="/wiki/South_Shetland_Islands" title="South Shetland Islands">South Shetlands</a> archipelago, landed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_George_Island_(Antarctica)" title="King George Island (Antarctica)">King George Island</a>, and claimed the new territories for <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>.</p>
<p>In the meantime, the Spanish Navy ship <i><a href="/wiki/San_Telmo_(ship)" title="San Telmo (ship)">San Telmo</a></i> sank in September 1819 when trying to cross Cape Horn. Parts of her wreckage were found months later by sealers on the north coast of <a href="/wiki/Livingston_Island" title="Livingston Island">Livingston Island</a> (<a href="/wiki/South_Shetland_Islands" title="South Shetland Islands">South Shetlands</a>). It is unknown if some survivor managed to be the first to set foot on these Antarctic islands.</p>
<p>The first confirmed sighting of mainland Antarctica cannot be accurately attributed to one single person. It can, however, be narrowed down to three individuals. According to various sources,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[46]</a></sup> three men all sighted the ice shelf or the continent within days or months of each other: von Bellingshausen, a captain in the Russian Imperial Navy; <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bransfield" title="Edward Bransfield">Edward Bransfield</a>, a captain in the British navy; and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Palmer" title="Nathaniel Palmer">Nathaniel Palmer</a>, an American sealer out of Stonington, Connecticut. It is certain that the expedition, led by von Bellingshausen and Lazarev on the ships <i>Vostok</i> and <i>Mirny</i>, reached a point within 32¬†km (20¬†mi) from <a href="/wiki/Princess_Martha_Coast" title="Princess Martha Coast">Princess Martha Coast</a> and recorded the sight of an ice shelf at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Southern_Ocean&amp;params=69_21_28_S_2_14_50_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69¬∞21‚Ä≤28‚Ä≥S</span> <span class="longitude">2¬∞14‚Ä≤50‚Ä≥W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">Ôªø / Ôªø</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.35778¬∞S 2.24722¬∞W</span><span style="display:none">Ôªø / <span class="geo">-69.35778; -2.24722</span></span></span></a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tammiksaar_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-tammiksaar-53">[47]</a></sup> that became known as the <a href="/wiki/Fimbul_Ice_Shelf" title="Fimbul Ice Shelf">Fimbul ice shelf</a>. On 30 January 1820, Bransfield sighted <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Peninsula" title="Trinity Peninsula">Trinity Peninsula</a>, the northernmost point of the Antarctic mainland, while Palmer sighted the mainland in the area south of Trinity Peninsula in November 1820. Von Bellingshausen's expedition also discovered <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_Island" title="Peter I Island">Peter I Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Island" title="Alexander Island">Alexander I Island</a>, the first islands to be discovered south of the circle.</p>
