<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antarctic_expeditions">Antarctic expeditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Antarctic expeditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_expeditions" title="List of Antarctic expeditions">List of Antarctic expeditions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vincennes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="623" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vincennes.jpg/220px-Vincennes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vincennes.jpg/330px-Vincennes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vincennes.jpg/440px-Vincennes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vincennes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>USS Vincennes at Disappointment Bay, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> in early 1840.</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3106" data-file-width="3096" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png/300px-Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png/450px-Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png/600px-Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polar_Regions_exploration_1911.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1911 South Polar Regions exploration map</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 1839, as part of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a> of 1838‚Äì42 conducted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> (sometimes called "the Wilkes Expedition"), an expedition sailed from Sydney, Australia, on the <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloops-of-war</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Vincennes_(1826)" title="USS Vincennes (1826)">USS¬†<i>Vincennes</i></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Peacock_(1828)" title="USS Peacock (1828)">USS¬†<i>Peacock</i></a> , the <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Porpoise_(1836)" title="USS Porpoise (1836)">USS¬†<i>Porpoise</i></a> , the <a href="/wiki/Full-rigged_ship" title="Full-rigged ship">full-rigged ship</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Relief_(1836)" title="USS Relief (1836)"><i>Relief</i></a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooners</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Sea_Gull_(1838)" title="USS Sea Gull (1838)"><i>Sea Gull</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Flying_Fish_(1838)" title="USS Flying Fish (1838)">USS¬†<i>Flying Fish</i></a> . They sailed into the Antarctic Ocean, as it was then known, and reported the discovery "of an Antarctic continent west of the <a href="/wiki/Balleny_Islands" title="Balleny Islands">Balleny Islands</a>" on 25 January 1840. That part of Antarctica was later named "<a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Land" title="Wilkes Land">Wilkes Land</a>", a name it maintains to this day.</p>
<p>Explorer <a href="/wiki/James_Clark_Ross" title="James Clark Ross">James Clark Ross</a> passed through what is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">Ross Sea</a> and discovered <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">Ross Island</a> (both of which were named for him) in 1841. He sailed along a huge wall of ice that was later named the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Ice_Shelf" title="Ross Ice Shelf">Ross Ice Shelf</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">Mount Erebus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Terror_(Antarctica)" title="Mount Terror (Antarctica)">Mount Terror</a> are named after two ships from his expedition: <a href="/wiki/HMS_Erebus_(1826)" title="HMS Erebus (1826)">HMS <i>Erebus</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Terror_(1813)" title="HMS Terror (1813)">Terror</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="578" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg/220px-Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg/330px-Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg/440px-Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Endurance_trapped_in_pack_ice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frank Hurley, <i>As time wore on it became more and more evident that the ship was doomed</i> (The <a href="/wiki/Endurance_(1912_ship)" title="Endurance (1912 ship)">Endurance</a> trapped in pack ice), <a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Australia" title="National Library of Australia">National Library of Australia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Trans-Antarctic_Expedition" title="Imperial Trans-Antarctic Expedition">Imperial Trans-Antarctic Expedition</a> of 1914, led by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">Ernest Shackleton</a>, set out to cross the continent via the pole, but their ship, the <a href="/wiki/Endurance_(1912_ship)" title="Endurance (1912 ship)"><i>Endurance</i></a>, was trapped and crushed by pack ice before they even landed. The expedition members survived after an epic journey on sledges over pack ice to <a href="/wiki/Elephant_Island" title="Elephant Island">Elephant Island</a>. Then Shackleton and five others crossed the Southern Ocean, in an open boat called <a href="/wiki/Voyage_of_the_James_Caird" title="Voyage of the James Caird"><i>James Caird</i></a>, and then trekked over <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a> to raise the alarm at the whaling station <a href="/wiki/Grytviken" title="Grytviken">Grytviken</a>.</p>
<p>In 1946, US Navy Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Byrd" title="Richard E. Byrd">Richard Evelyn Byrd</a> and more than 4,700 military personnel visited the Antarctic in an expedition called <a href="/wiki/Operation_Highjump" title="Operation Highjump">Operation <i>Highjump</i></a>. Reported to the public as a scientific mission, the details were kept secret and it may have actually been a training or testing mission for the military. The expedition was, in both military or scientific planning terms, put together very quickly. The group contained an unusually high amount of military equipment, including an aircraft carrier, submarines, military support ships, assault troops and military vehicles. The expedition was planned to last for eight months but was unexpectedly terminated after only two months. With the exception of some eccentric entries in Admiral Byrd's diaries, no real explanation for the early termination has ever been officially given.</p>
<p>Captain <a href="/wiki/Finn_Ronne" title="Finn Ronne">Finn Ronne</a>, Byrd's executive officer, returned to Antarctica with his own expedition in 1947‚Äì1948, with Navy support, three planes, and dogs. Ronne disproved the notion that the continent was divided in two and established that East and West Antarctica was one single continent, i.e. that the Weddell Sea and the Ross Sea are not connected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> The expedition explored and mapped large parts of Palmer Land and the Weddell Sea coastline, and identified the <a href="/wiki/Filchner-Ronne_Ice_Shelf" title="Filchner-Ronne Ice Shelf">Ronne Ice Shelf</a>, named by Ronne after his wife <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Ronne" title="Jackie Ronne">Edith "Jackie" Ronne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup> Ronne covered 3,600 miles by ski and dog sled‚Äîmore than any other explorer in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ronne_Antarctic_Research_Expedition" title="Ronne Antarctic Research Expedition">Ronne Antarctic Research Expedition</a> discovered and mapped the last unknown coastline in the world and was the first Antarctic expedition to ever include women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Recent history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="1000" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg/220px-Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg/330px-Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg/440px-Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_and_Explorer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
MS <i>Explorer</i> in Antarctica in January 1999. She sank on 23 November 2007 after hitting an <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">iceberg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty</a> was signed on 1 December 1959 and came into force on 23 June 1961. Among other provisions, this treaty limits <a href="/wiki/Military_activity_in_the_Antarctic" title="Military activity in the Antarctic">military activity in the Antarctic</a> to the support of scientific research.</p>
<p>The first person to sail single-handed to Antarctica was the New Zealander <a href="/wiki/David_Henry_Lewis" title="David Henry Lewis">David Henry Lewis</a>, in 1972, in a 10-metre steel sloop <i>Ice Bird</i>.</p>
<p>A baby, named <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Palma" title="Emilio Palma">Emilio Marcos de Palma</a>, was born near <a href="/wiki/Hope_Bay" title="Hope Bay">Hope Bay</a> on 7 January 1978, becoming the first baby born on the continent. He also was born further south than anyone in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/MV_Explorer_(1969)" title="MV Explorer (1969)">MS Explorer</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a> operated by the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Lars-Eric_Lindblad" title="Lars-Eric Lindblad">Lars-Eric Lindblad</a>. Observers point to the <i>Explorer's</i> 1969 expeditionary cruise to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> as the frontrunner for today's sea-based tourism in that region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> The <i>Explorer</i> was the first cruise ship used specifically to sail the icy waters of the Antarctic Ocean and the first to sink there<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[56]</a></sup> when she struck an unidentified submerged object on 23 November 2007, reported to be ice, which caused a 10 by 4 inches (25 by 10¬†cm) gash in the hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News-63">[57]</a></sup> The <i>Explorer</i> was abandoned in the early hours of 23 November 2007 after taking on water near the <a href="/wiki/South_Shetland_Islands" title="South Shetland Islands">South Shetland Islands</a> in the Southern Ocean, an area which is usually stormy but was calm at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup> The <i>Explorer</i> was confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Navy" title="Chilean Navy">Chilean Navy</a> to have sunk at approximately position: 62¬∞ 24‚Ä≤ South, 57¬∞ 16‚Ä≤ West,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> in roughly 600¬†m of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_and_subantarctic_islands" title="List of Antarctic and subantarctic islands">List of Antarctic and subantarctic islands</a></div>
<p>The Southern Ocean, geologically the youngest of the oceans, was formed when Antarctica and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> moved apart, opening the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a>, roughly 30 million years ago. The separation of the continents allowed the formation of the Antarctic Circumpolar Current.</p>
<p>With a northern limit at <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60¬∞S</a>, the Southern Ocean differs from the other oceans in that its largest boundary, the northern boundary, does not abut a landmass (as it did with the first edition of <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>). Instead, the northern limit is with the Atlantic, Indian and Pacific Oceans.</p>
<p>One reason for considering it as a separate ocean stems from the fact that much of the water of the Southern Ocean differs from the water in the other oceans. Water gets transported around the Southern Ocean fairly rapidly because of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> which circulates around Antarctica. Water in the Southern Ocean south of, for example, New Zealand, resembles the water in the Southern Ocean south of South America more closely than it resembles the water in the Pacific Ocean.</p>
<p>The Southern Ocean has typical depths of between 4,000 and 5,000¬†m (13,000 and 16,000¬†ft) over most of its extent with only limited areas of shallow water. The Southern Ocean's greatest depth of 7,236¬†m (23,740¬†ft) occurs at the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/South_Sandwich_Trench" title="South Sandwich Trench">South Sandwich Trench</a>, at 60¬∞00'S, 024¬∞W. The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_continental_shelf" title="Antarctic continental shelf">Antarctic continental shelf</a> appears generally narrow and unusually deep, its edge lying at depths up to 800¬†m (2,600¬†ft), compared to a global mean of 133¬†m (436¬†ft).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">Equinox</a> to equinox in line with the sun's seasonal influence, the Antarctic ice pack fluctuates from an average minimum of 2.6¬†million square kilometres (1.0<span style="margin-left:0.2em">√ó<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>¬†sq¬†mi) in March to about 18.8¬†million square kilometres (7.3<span style="margin-left:0.2em">√ó<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>¬†sq¬†mi) in September, more than a sevenfold increase in area.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-divisions_of_the_Southern_Ocean">Sub-divisions of the Southern Ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sub-divisions of the Southern Ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sub-divisions of oceans are geographical features such as "seas", "straits", "bays", "channels", and "gulfs". There are many sub-divisions of the Southern Ocean defined in the never-approved 2002 draft fourth edition of the IHO publication <i>Limits of Oceans and Seas</i>. In clockwise order these include (with IHO sub-division chartlet numbers in parenthesis) the <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">Weddell Sea</a> (10.1), the <a href="/wiki/Lazarev_Sea" title="Lazarev Sea">Lazarev Sea</a> (10.2), the <a href="/wiki/Riiser-Larsen_Sea" title="Riiser-Larsen Sea">Riiser-Larsen Sea</a> (10.3), the <a href="/wiki/Cosmonauts_Sea" title="Cosmonauts Sea">Cosmonauts Sea</a> (10.4), the <a href="/wiki/Cooperation_Sea" title="Cooperation Sea">Cooperation Sea</a> (10.5), the <a href="/wiki/Davis_Sea" title="Davis Sea">Davis Sea</a> (10.6), <a href="/wiki/Davis_Sea" title="Davis Sea">Tryoshinikova Gulf</a> (10.6.1), the <a href="/wiki/Mawson_Sea" title="Mawson Sea">Mawson Sea</a> (10.7), the <a href="/wiki/D%27Urville_Sea" title="D'Urville Sea">Dumont D'Urville Sea</a> (10.8), the <a href="/wiki/Somov_Sea" title="Somov Sea">Somov Sea</a> (10.9), the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">Ross Sea</a> (10.10), <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Sound" title="McMurdo Sound">McMurdo Sound</a> (10.10.1), the <a href="/wiki/Amundsen_Sea" title="Amundsen Sea">Amundsen Sea</a> (10.11), the <a href="/wiki/Bellingshausen_Sea" title="Bellingshausen Sea">Bellingshausen Sea</a> (10.12), part of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> (10.13), <a href="/wiki/Bransfield_Strait" title="Bransfield Strait">Bransfield Strait</a> (10.14) and part of the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Sea" title="Scotia Sea">Scotia Sea</a> (4.2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO2002_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHO2002-34">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[note 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[note 8]</a></sup> A number of these such as the 2002 Russian-proposed "Consmonauts Sea", "Cooperation Sea", and "Somov (mid-1950s Russian polar explorer) Sea" are not included in the 1953 IHO document which remains currently in force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-32">[26]</a></sup> because they received their names largely originated from 1962 onward. Leading geographic authorities and atlases do not use these latter three names, including the 2014 10th edition World Atlas from the United States' <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a> and the 2014 12th edition of the British <a href="/wiki/Times_Atlas_of_the_World" title="Times Atlas of the World">Times Atlas of the World</a>, but Soviet and Russian-issued maps do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="614" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg/220px-McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg/330px-McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg/440px-McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:McMurdo_Sound_Glacier-iv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An iceberg being pushed out of a shipping lane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Burton_Island_(AGB-1)" title="USS Burton Island (AGB-1)">USS¬†<i>Burton Island</i>¬†(AGB-1)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Atka_(AGB-3)" title="USS Atka (AGB-3)">USS¬†<i>Atka</i>¬†(AGB-3)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/USS_Glacier_(AGB-4)" title="USS Glacier (AGB-4)">USS¬†<i>Glacier</i>¬†(AGB-4)</a> pushing an iceberg out of a channel in the "Silent Land" near <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">McMurdo Station</a>, Antarctica, 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources">Natural resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Natural resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manganknolle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1238" data-file-width="1904" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Manganknolle.jpg/220px-Manganknolle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Manganknolle.jpg/330px-Manganknolle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Manganknolle.jpg/440px-Manganknolle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manganknolle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Manganese nodule</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Southern Ocean probably contains large, and possibly giant, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a> fields on the <a href="/wiki/Continental_margin" title="Continental margin">continental margin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Placer_deposit" title="Placer deposit">Placer deposits</a>, accumulation of valuable minerals such as gold, formed by gravity separation during sedimentary processes are also expected to exist in the Southern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAgeo_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAgeo-10">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manganese_nodules" title="Manganese nodules">Manganese nodules</a> are expected to exist in the Southern Ocean. Manganese nodules are rock <a href="/wiki/Concretion" title="Concretion">concretions</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a> bottom formed of concentric layers of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a> around a core. The core may be microscopically small and is sometimes completely transformed into manganese minerals by <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a>. Interest in the potential exploitation of polymetallic nodules generated a great deal of activity among prospective mining consortia in the 1960s and 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAgeo_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIAgeo-10">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">icebergs</a> that form each year around in the Southern Ocean hold enough <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> to meet the needs of every person on Earth for several months. For several decades there have been proposals, none yet to be feasible or successful, to tow Southern Ocean icebergs to more arid northern regions (such as Australia) where they can be harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_hazards">Natural hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Natural hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">Icebergs</a> can occur at any time of year throughout the ocean. Some may have drafts up to several hundred meters; smaller icebergs, iceberg fragments and sea-ice (generally 0.5 to 1 m thick) also pose problems for ships. The deep continental shelf has a floor of glacial deposits varying widely over short distances.</p>
<p>Sailors know latitudes from <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40</a> to <a href="/wiki/70th_parallel_south" title="70th parallel south">70 degrees south</a> as the "<a href="/wiki/Roaring_Forties" title="Roaring Forties">Roaring Forties</a>", "Furious Fifties" and "Shrieking Sixties" due to high winds and large waves that form as winds blow around the entire globe unimpeded by any land-mass. Icebergs, especially in May to October, make the area even more dangerous. The remoteness of the region makes sources of search and rescue scarce.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_oceanography">Physical oceanography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Physical oceanography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="966" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg/250px-Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg/375px-Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg/500px-Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Antarctic Circumpolar Current (ACC) is the strongest current system in the world oceans, linking the Atlantic, Indian and Pacific basins.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current_and_Antarctic_Convergence">Antarctic Circumpolar Current and Antarctic Convergence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Antarctic Circumpolar Current and Antarctic Convergence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> moves perpetually eastward ‚Äî chasing and joining itself, and at 21,000¬†km (13,000¬†mi) in length ‚Äî it comprises the world's longest ocean current, transporting 130¬†million cubic metres per second (4.6<span style="margin-left:0.2em">√ó<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>¬†cu¬†ft/s) of water ‚Äì 100 times the flow of all the world's rivers.</p>
<p>Several processes operate along the coast of Antarctica to produce, in the Southern Ocean, types of <a href="/wiki/Water_mass" title="Water mass">water masses</a> not produced elsewhere in the oceans of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. One of these is the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Bottom_Water" title="Antarctic Bottom Water">Antarctic Bottom Water</a>, a very cold, highly saline, dense water that forms under <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a>.</p>
<p>Associated with the Circumpolar Current is the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a> encircling Antarctica, where cold northward-flowing Antarctic waters meet the relatively warmer waters of the <a href="/wiki/Subantarctic" title="Subantarctic">subantarctic</a>, Antarctic waters predominantly sink beneath subantarctic waters, while associated zones of mixing and <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> create a zone very high in nutrients. These nurture high levels of <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> with associated copepods and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">Antarctic krill</a>, and resultant foodchains supporting fish, whales, seals, penguins, albatrosses and a wealth of other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Antarctic Convergence is considered to be the best natural definition of the northern extent of the Southern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAgeo_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIAgeo-10">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_bottom_water.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="745" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Antarctic_bottom_water.svg/250px-Antarctic_bottom_water.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Antarctic_bottom_water.svg/375px-Antarctic_bottom_water.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Antarctic_bottom_water.svg/500px-Antarctic_bottom_water.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_bottom_water.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Upwelling in the Southern Ocean</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upwelling">Upwelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Upwelling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large-scale <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> is found in the Southern Ocean. Strong westerly (eastward) winds blow around <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, driving a significant flow of water northwards. This is actually a type of coastal upwelling. Since there are no continents in a band of open latitudes between <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">Antarctic Peninsula</a>, some of this water is drawn up from great depths. In many numerical models and observational syntheses, the Southern Ocean upwelling represents the primary means by which deep dense water is brought to the surface. Shallower, wind-driven upwelling is also found off the west coasts of North and South America, northwest and southwest Africa, and southwest and <a href="/wiki/Great_South_Australian_Coastal_Upwelling_System" title="Great South Australian Coastal Upwelling System">southeast Australia</a>, all associated with oceanic subtropical high pressure circulations.</p>
<p>Some models of the ocean circulation suggest that broad-scale upwelling occurs in the tropics, as pressure driven flows converge water toward the low latitudes where it is diffusively warmed from above. The required diffusion coefficients, however, appear to be larger than are observed in the real ocean. Nonetheless, some diffusive upwelling does probably occur.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="765" data-file-width="750" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png/250px-Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png/375px-Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png/500px-Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_frontal-system_hg.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Location of the Southern Ocean gyres.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ross_and_Weddell_Gyres">Ross and Weddell Gyres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ross and Weddell Gyres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ross_Gyre" title="Ross Gyre">Ross Gyre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Gyre" title="Weddell Gyre">Weddell Gyre</a> are two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_gyre" title="Oceanic gyre">gyres</a> that exist within the Southern Ocean. The gyres are located in the <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">Ross Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">Weddell Sea</a> respectively, and both rotate clockwise. The gyres are formed by interactions between the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Continental_Shelf" title="Antarctic Continental Shelf">Antarctic Continental Shelf</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">Sea ice</a> has been noted to persist in the central area of the Ross Gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[65]</a></sup> There is some evidence that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> has resulted in some decrease of the <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of the waters of the Ross Gyre since the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a> acting to the left in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Ekman_transport" title="Ekman transport">Ekman transport</a> away from the centres of the Weddell Gyre, these regions are very productive due to upwelling of cold, nutrient rich water.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sea temperatures vary from about ‚àí2 to 10¬†<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">¬∞C</a> (28 to 50 <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">¬∞F</a>). Cyclonic storms travel eastward around the continent and frequently become intense because of the temperature contrast between ice and <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_zone" title="Pelagic zone">open ocean</a>. The ocean-area from about <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">latitude 40 south</a> to the Antarctic Circle has the strongest average winds found anywhere on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[67]</a></sup> In winter the ocean freezes outward to <a href="/wiki/65th_parallel_south" title="65th parallel south">65 degrees south latitude</a> in the Pacific sector and <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55 degrees south latitude</a> in the Atlantic sector, lowering surface temperatures well below 0 degrees Celsius. At some coastal points, however, persistent intense drainage winds from the interior keep the shoreline ice-free throughout the winter.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:2008px;">

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_ecozone" title="Antarctic ecozone">Antarctic ecozone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_microorganism" title="Antarctic microorganism">Antarctic microorganism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Antarctica" title="Wildlife of Antarctica">Wildlife of Antarctica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">

<a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">Orca</a> (<i>Orcinus orca</i>) hunting a <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seal</a> in the Southern Ocean.</div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A variety of marine animals exist and rely, directly or indirectly, on the phytoplankton in the Southern Ocean. Antarctic sea life includes <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">orcas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colossal_squid" title="Colossal squid">colossal squids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">fur seals</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a> is the only penguin that breeds during the winter in Antarctica, while the <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_penguin" title="Ad√©lie penguin">Ad√©lie penguin</a> breeds farther south than any other penguin. The <a href="/wiki/Rockhopper_penguin" title="Rockhopper penguin">rockhopper penguin</a> has distinctive feathers around the eyes, giving the appearance of elaborate eyelashes. <a href="/wiki/King_penguin" title="King penguin">King penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinstrap_penguin" title="Chinstrap penguin">chinstrap penguins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gentoo_penguin" title="Gentoo penguin">gentoo penguins</a> also breed in the Antarctic.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_fur_seal" title="Antarctic fur seal">Antarctic fur seal</a> was very heavily hunted in the 18th and 19th centuries for its pelt by sealers from the United States and the United Kingdom. The <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seal</a>, a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_seal" title="True seal">true seal</a>", is named after <a href="/wiki/James_Weddell" title="James Weddell">Sir James Weddell</a>, commander of British sealing expeditions in the <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">Weddell Sea</a>. <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">Antarctic krill</a>, which congregates in large <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> of the Southern Ocean, and is an important food organism for whales, seals, <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seals</a>, fur seals, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Notothenioidei" title="Notothenioidei">icefish</a>, penguins, <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a> and many other birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> communities of the seafloor are diverse and dense, with up to 155,000 animals found in 1 square metre (10.8¬†sq¬†ft). As the seafloor environment is very similar all around the Antarctic, hundreds of species can be found all the way around the mainland, which is a uniquely wide distribution for such a large community. <a href="/wiki/Deep-sea_gigantism" title="Deep-sea gigantism">Deep-sea gigantism</a> is common among these animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseabed_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADseabed-77">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>A census of sea life carried out during the <a href="/wiki/International_Polar_Year" title="International Polar Year">International Polar Year</a> and which involved some 500 researchers was released in 2010. The research is part of the global <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Marine_Life" title="Census of Marine Life">Census of Marine Life</a> (CoML) and has disclosed some remarkable findings. More than 235 marine organisms live in both polar regions, having bridged the gap of 12,000¬†km (7,456¬†mi). Large animals such as some cetaceans and birds make the round trip annually. More surprising are small forms of life such as mudworms, <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a> and free-swimming snails found in both polar oceans. Various factors may aid in their distribution ‚Äì fairly uniform temperatures of the deep ocean at the poles and the equator which differ by no more than 5¬†¬∞C, and the major current systems or marine <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">conveyor belt</a> which transport egg and larva stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wonder_albat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1536" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Wonder_albat.jpg/220px-Wonder_albat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Wonder_albat.jpg/330px-Wonder_albat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Wonder_albat.jpg/440px-Wonder_albat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wonder_albat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Wandering_albatross" title="Wandering albatross">wandering albatross</a> (<i>Diomedea exulans</i>) on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Georgia" title="South Georgia">South Georgia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_birds_of_Antarctica" title="List of birds of Antarctica">List of birds of Antarctica</a></div>
<p>The rocky shores of mainland Antarctica and its offshore islands provide nesting space for over 100 million birds every spring. These nesters include species of <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">terns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADbirds_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADbirds-79">[71]</a></sup> The insectivorous <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_pipit" title="South Georgia pipit">South Georgia pipit</a> is <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Georgia" title="South Georgia">South Georgia</a> and some smaller surrounding islands. Freshwater ducks inhabit South Georgia and the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Islands" title="Kerguelen Islands">Kerguelen Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BASanimals_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-BASanimals-80">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The flightless <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a> are all located in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, with the greatest concentration located on and around Antarctica. Four of the 18 penguin species live and breed on the mainland and its close offshore islands. Another four species live on the subantarctic islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADpenguins_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADpenguins-81">[73]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_penguins" title="Emperor penguins">Emperor penguins</a> have four overlapping layers of feathers, keeping them warm. They are the only Antarctic animal to breed during the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADadapt_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADadapt-82">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Icefishuk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Icefishuk.jpg/220px-Icefishuk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Icefishuk.jpg/330px-Icefishuk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Icefishuk.jpg/440px-Icefishuk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Icefishuk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fish of the <a href="/wiki/Notothenioidei" title="Notothenioidei">Notothenioidei</a> suborder, such as this young icefish, are mostly endemic to Antarctica.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are very few species of fish in the Southern Ocean. The <a href="/wiki/Channichthyidae" title="Channichthyidae">Channichthyidae</a> family, also known as white-blooded fish, are only found in the Southern Ocean. They lack <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemoglobin" title="Haemoglobin">haemoglobin</a> in their blood, resulting in their blood being colourless. One Channichthyidae species, the <a href="/wiki/Mackerel_icefish" title="Mackerel icefish">mackerel icefish</a> (<i>Champsocephalus gunnari</i>), was once the most common fish in coastal waters less than 400 metres (1,312¬†ft) deep, but was <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfished</a> in the 1970s and 1980s. Schools of icefish spend the day at the seafloor and the night higher in the <a href="/wiki/Water_column" title="Water column">water column</a> eating plankton and smaller fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfish_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADfish-83">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two species from the <i><a href="/wiki/Dissostichus" title="Dissostichus">Dissostichus</a></i> genus, the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_toothfish" title="Antarctic toothfish">Antarctic toothfish</a> (<i>Dissostichus mawsoni</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a> (<i>Dissostichus eleginoides</i>). These two species live on the seafloor 100‚Äì3,000 metres (328‚Äì9,843¬†ft) deep, and can grow to around 2 metres (7¬†ft) long weighing up to 100 kilograms (220¬†lb), living up to 45 years. The Antarctic toothfish lives close to the Antarctic mainland, whereas the Patagonian toothfish lives in the relatively warmer subantarctic waters. Due to the low water temperatures around the mainland, the Antarctic toothfish has <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze_protein" title="Antifreeze protein">antifreeze proteins</a> in its blood and tissues. Toothfish are commercially fished, and illegal overfishing has reduced toothfish populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfish_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-AADfish-83">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another abundant fish group is the <i><a href="/wiki/Notothenia" title="Notothenia">Notothenia</a></i> genus, which like the Antarctic toothfish have antifreeze in their bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfish_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-AADfish-83">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="800" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg/220px-Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg/330px-Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg/440px-Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phoque_de_Weddell_-_Weddell_Seal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seals</a> (<i>Leptonychotes weddellii</i>) are the most southerly of Antarctic mammals.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_mammals_of_Antarctica" title="List of mammals of Antarctica">List of mammals of Antarctica</a></div>
<p>Seven <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">pinniped</a> species inhabit Antarctica. The largest, the <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">elephant seal</a> (<i>Mirounga leonina</i>), can reach up to 4,000 kilograms (8,818¬†lb), while females of the smallest, the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_fur_seal" title="Antarctic fur seal">Antarctic fur seal</a> (<i>Arctocephalus gazella</i>), reach only 150 kilograms (331¬†lb). These two species live north of the sea ice, and breed in <a href="/wiki/Harem_(zoology)" title="Harem (zoology)">harems</a> on beaches. The other four species can live on the sea ice. <a href="/wiki/Crabeater_seal" title="Crabeater seal">Crabeater seals</a> (<i>Lobodon carcinophagus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seals</a> (<i>Leptonychotes weddellii</i>) form breeding colonies, whereas <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seals</a> (<i>Hydrurga leptonyx</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ross_seal" title="Ross seal">Ross seals</a> (<i>Ommatophoca rossii</i>) live solitary lives. Although these species hunt underwater, they breed on land or ice and spend a great deal of time there, as they have no terrestrial predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseals_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADseals-84">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The four species that inhabit sea ice are thought to make up 50% of the total biomass of the world's seals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADicespecies_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADicespecies-85">[77]</a></sup> Crabeater seals have a population of around 15 million, making them one of the most numerous large animals on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADcrabeater_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADcrabeater-86">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_sea_lion" title="New Zealand sea lion">New Zealand sea lion</a> (<i>Phocarctos hookeri</i>), one of the rarest and most localised pinnipeds, breeds almost exclusively on the subantarctic <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Islands" title="Auckland Islands">Auckland Islands</a>, although historically it had a wider range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADsealion_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADsealion-87">[79]</a></sup> Out of all permanent mammalian residents, the Weddell seals live the furthest south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADweddell_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADweddell-88">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 10 <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetacean</a> species found in the Southern Ocean; six <a href="/wiki/Baleen_whale" title="Baleen whale">baleen whales</a>, and four <a href="/wiki/Toothed_whale" title="Toothed whale">toothed whales</a>. The largest of these, the <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a> (<i>Balaenoptera musculus</i>), grows to 24 metres (79¬†ft) long weighing 84 tonnes. Many of these species are <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migratory</a>, and travel to <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> waters during the Antarctic winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADwhales_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADwhales-89">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_krill_(Euphausia_superba).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1269" data-file-width="1869" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg/220px-Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg/330px-Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg/440px-Antarctic_krill_%28Euphausia_superba%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_krill_(Euphausia_superba).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">Antarctic krill</a> (<i>Euphausia superba</i>) are a <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a> of the food web.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arthropods">Arthropods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Arthropods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Five species of <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a>, small free-swimming <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, are found in the Southern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADmagic_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADmagic-90">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">Antarctic krill</a> (<i>Euphausia superba</i>) is one of the most abundant animal species on earth, with a <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> of around 500 million tonnes. Each individual is 6 centimetres (2.4¬†in) long and weighs over 1 gram (0.035¬†oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADkrill_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADkrill-91">[83]</a></sup> The swarms that form can stretch for kilometres, with up to 30,000 individuals per 1 cubic metre (35¬†cu¬†ft), turning the water red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADmagic_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-AADmagic-90">[82]</a></sup> Swarms usually remain in deep water during the day, ascending during the night to feed on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>. Many larger animals depend on krill for their own survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADkrill_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-AADkrill-91">[83]</a></sup> During the winter when food is scarce, adult Antarctic krill can revert to a smaller juvenile stage, using their own body as nutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADmagic_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-AADmagic-90">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many benthic crustaceans have a non-seasonal breeding cycle, and some raise their young in a <a href="/wiki/Brood_pouch_(Peracarida)" title="Brood pouch (Peracarida)">brood pouch</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Glyptonotus_antarcticus" title="Glyptonotus antarcticus">Glyptonotus antarcticus</a></i> is an unusually large benthic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopod" title="Isopod">isopod</a>, reaching 20 centimetres (8¬†in) in length weighing 70 grams (2.47¬†oz). <a href="/wiki/Amphipoda" title="Amphipoda">Amphipods</a> are abundant in soft sediments, eating a range of items, from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> to other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseabed_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-AADseabed-77">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow moving <a href="/wiki/Sea_spider" title="Sea spider">sea spiders</a> are common, sometimes growing as large as a human hand. They feed on the <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bryozoa" title="Bryozoa">bryozoans</a> that litter the seabed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseabed_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-AADseabed-77">[69]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="842" data-file-width="1000" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg/220px-Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg/330px-Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg/440px-Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Onykia_ingens_384_mm_ML.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moroteuthis_ingens" title="Moroteuthis ingens">warty squid</a> (<i>Moroteuthis ingens</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_animals" title="Aquatic animals">aquatic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> are present in Antarctica. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalves" title="Bivalves">Bivalves</a> such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adamussium_colbecki" title="Adamussium colbecki">Adamussium colbecki</a></i> move around on the seafloor, while others such as <i><a href="/wiki/Laternula_elliptica" title="Laternula elliptica">Laternula elliptica</a></i> live in burrows <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filtering</a> the water above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseabed_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-AADseabed-77">[69]</a></sup> There are around 70 <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopod</a> species in the Southern Ocean, the largest of which is the <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a> (<i>Architeuthis</i> sp.), which at 15 metres (49¬†ft) is the largest invertebrate in the world. <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">Squid</a> makes up the entire diet of some animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Grey-headed_albatross" title="Grey-headed albatross">grey-headed albatrosses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whales</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moroteuthis_ingens" title="Moroteuthis ingens">warty squid</a> (<i>Moroteuthis ingens</i>) is one of the subantarctic's most preyed upon species by vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADsquid_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADsquid-92">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchin</a> genus <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Abatus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Abatus (page does not exist)">Abatus</a></i> burrow through the sediment eating the nutrients they find in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADseabed_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-AADseabed-77">[69]</a></sup> Two species of <a href="/wiki/Salp" title="Salp">salps</a> are common in Antarctic waters, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Salpa_thompsoni&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Salpa thompsoni (page does not exist)">Salpa thompsoni</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ihlea_racovitzai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ihlea racovitzai (page does not exist)">Ihlea racovitzai</a></i>. <i>Salpa thompsoni</i> is found in ice-free areas, whereas <i>Ihlea racovitzai</i> is found in the high latitude areas near ice. Due to their low nutritional value, they are normally only eaten by fish, with larger animals such as birds and marine mammals only eating them when other food is scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADsalps_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADsalps-93">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a> are long lived, and sensitive to environmental changes due to the specificity of the <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> microbial communities within them. As a result, they function as indicators of environmental health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADsponges_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADsponges-94">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_issues">Current issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Current issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Increased solar <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet radiation</a> resulting from the Antarctic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_hole" title="Ozone hole">ozone hole</a> has reduced marine primary productivity (<a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>) by as much as 15% and has started damaging the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> of some fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup> Illegal, unreported, and unregulated fishing, especially the landing of an estimated five to six times more <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a> than the regulated fishery, likely affects the sustainability of the stock. Long-line fishing for toothfish causes a high incidence of seabird mortality.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_agreements">International agreements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: International agreements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All international agreements regarding the world's oceans apply to the Southern Ocean. In addition, it is subject to these agreements specific to the region:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean_Whale_Sanctuary" title="Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary">Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a> (IWC) prohibits commercial <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a> south of <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40 degrees south</a> (south of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60 degrees south</a> between <a href="/wiki/50th_meridian_west" title="50th meridian west">50 degrees</a> and <a href="/wiki/130th_meridian_west" title="130th meridian west">130 degrees west</a>). <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> regularly does not recognize this provision, because the sanctuary violates IWC charter. Since the scope of the sanctuary is limited to commercial whaling, in regard to its whaling permit and whaling for scientific research, a Japanese fleet carried out an annual whale-hunt in the region. On 31 March 2014, the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> ruled that Japan's whaling program, which Japan has long claimed is for scientific purposes, was a cloak for commercial whaling, and no further permits would be granted.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Convention_for_the_Conservation_of_Antarctic_Seals" title="Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Seals">Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Seals</a></i> is part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty System</a></i>. It was signed at the conclusion of a multilateral conference in London on 11 February 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Convention_for_the_Conservation_of_Antarctic_Marine_Living_Resources" title="Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources">Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources</a></i> (CCAMLR) is part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty System</a></i>. The Convention was entered into force on 7 April 1982 and has its goal is to preserve <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">marine life</a> and environmental integrity in and near <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. It was established in large part to concerns that an increase in <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a> catches in the Southern Ocean could have a serious impact on populations of other marine life which are dependent upon krill for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Many nations prohibit the exploration for and the exploitation of <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> resources south of the fluctuating <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">Antarctic Convergence</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> which lies in the middle of the Antarctic Circumpolar Current and serves as the dividing line between the very cold polar surface waters to the south and the warmer waters to the north. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" title="Antarctic Treaty">Antarctic Treaty</a> covers the portion of the globe south of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">sixty degrees south</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup> it prohibits new claims to Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources</i> applies to the area south of 60¬∞ South latitude as well as the areas further north up to the limit of the Antarctic Convergence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Between 1 July 1998 and 30 June 1999, fisheries landed 119,898 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, of which 85% consisted of <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a> and 14% of <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a>. International agreements came into force in late 1999 to reduce illegal, unreported, and unregulated fishing, which in the 1998‚Äì99 season landed five to six times more Patagonian toothfish than the regulated fishery.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ports_and_harbors">Ports and harbors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Ports and harbors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="918" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg/300px-USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg/450px-USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg/600px-USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USNS_Southern_Cross_at_the_ice_pier_in_1983.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Severe cracks in an <a href="/wiki/Ice_pier" title="Ice pier">ice pier</a> in use for four seasons at <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">McMurdo Station</a> slowed cargo operations in 1983 and proved a safety hazard.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Major operational ports include: <a href="/wiki/Rothera_Research_Station" title="Rothera Research Station">Rothera Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmer_Station" title="Palmer Station">Palmer Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Villa_Las_Estrellas" title="Villa Las Estrellas">Villa Las Estrellas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esperanza_Base" title="Esperanza Base">Esperanza Base</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mawson_Station" title="Mawson Station">Mawson Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">McMurdo Station</a>, and offshore anchorages in Antarctica.</p>
<p>Few ports or harbors exist on the southern (Antarctic) coast of the Southern Ocean, since ice conditions limit use of most shores to short periods in midsummer; even then some require <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreaker</a> escort for access. Most Antarctic ports are operated by government research stations and, except in an emergency, remain closed to commercial or private vessels; vessels in any port south of <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60 degrees south</a> are subject to inspection by Antarctic Treaty observers.</p>
<p>The Southern Ocean's southernmost port operates at McMurdo Station at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Southern_Ocean&amp;params=77_50_S_166_40_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">77¬∞50‚Ä≤S</span> <span class="longitude">166¬∞40‚Ä≤E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">Ôªø / Ôªø</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">77.833¬∞S 166.667¬∞E</span><span style="display:none">Ôªø / <span class="geo">-77.833; 166.667</span></span></span></a></span>. <a href="/wiki/Winter_Quarters_Bay" title="Winter Quarters Bay">Winter Quarters Bay</a> forms a small harbor, on the southern tip of <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">Ross Island</a> where a floating <a href="/wiki/Ice_pier" title="Ice pier">ice pier</a> makes port operations possible in summer. <a href="/wiki/Operation_Deep_Freeze" title="Operation Deep Freeze">Operation Deep Freeze</a> personnel constructed the first ice pier at McMurdo in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unique_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unique-102">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Based on the original 1928 IHO delineation of the Southern Ocean (and the 1937 delineation if the <a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a> is considered integral), Australian ports and harbors between <a href="/wiki/Cape_Leeuwin" title="Cape Leeuwin">Cape Leeuwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Otway" title="Cape Otway">Cape Otway</a> on the Australian mainland and along the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> would also be identified as ports and harbors existing in the Southern Ocean. These would include the larger ports and harbors of <a href="/wiki/Albany,_Western_Australia" title="Albany, Western Australia">Albany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thevenard,_South_Australia" title="Thevenard, South Australia">Thevenard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Port_Lincoln" title="Port Lincoln">Port Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whyalla" title="Whyalla">Whyalla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_Augusta,_South_Australia" title="Port Augusta, South Australia">Port Augusta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Port_Adelaide" title="Port Adelaide">Port Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Victoria" title="Portland, Victoria">Portland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warrnambool" title="Warrnambool">Warrnambool</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Harbour" title="Macquarie Harbour">Macquarie Harbour</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yacht_race" title="Yacht race">Yacht races</a> have been held in the Southern Ocean, such as the <a href="/wiki/Volvo_Ocean_Race" title="Volvo Ocean Race">Volvo Ocean Race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Velux_5_Oceans_Race" title="Velux 5 Oceans Race">Velux 5 Oceans Race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vend%C3%A9e_Globe" title="Vend√©e Globe">Vend√©e Globe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne_Trophy" title="Jules Verne Trophy">Jules Verne Trophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_Challenge" title="Global Challenge">Global Challenge</a>.</p>
